> Oh and here is what chatGPT says the religion is, Absolutely — here’s a “go all out” write-up you can hand
  straight to Claude Code as your website source text. I’m going to write it like a comprehensive doctrine/overview
   page plus some structured subsections that can be turned into navigation.

  ---

  # Lifeism (命道教 / Order of Life): a religion of universal flourishing, negative theology polytheism, and the
  moral horizon of immortality

  ## 1) What Lifeism is

  **Lifeism** is a religious–philosophical system centered on a single foundational commitment:

  > **Life is the primary value, and the work of intelligence is to expand life’s freedom, depth, continuity, and
  possibility.**

  Lifeism is not “life at any cost” in a crude sense. It is a disciplined ethics and metaphysics built around **the
   preservation and expansion of meaningful agency**—the capacity of living beings (and future beings) to
  experience, choose, learn, create, relate, remember, and become.

  Lifeism treats the universe as a stage on which life can either be **constrained** (by suffering, scarcity,
  ignorance, coercion, death) or **liberated** (through knowledge, compassion, technology, governance, and
  spiritual maturity). Its religious character comes from taking life seriously enough that the project becomes not
   merely political or scientific, but **sacred**: a long civilizational vow.

  It is simultaneously:

  * **A moral project**: reducing involuntary suffering, expanding autonomy, building conditions for flourishing.
  * **A metaphysical stance**: reality is deeper than any single description; the sacred is real but not
  capturable.
  * **A practical program**: institutions, tools, and technologies should be designed to promote life’s continuity
  and freedom.
  * **A spiritual discipline**: cultivating honesty, reverence, and responsibility in the face of vast unknowns.

  Lifeism is compatible with many cultural forms. It can be lived as a minimalist personal ethic, or as a full
  ritualized religion with liturgy, symbolism, calendars, devotional practice, and sacred art.

  ---

  ## 2) The core commitments (the “axis” of Lifeism)

  Lifeism can be summarized as a set of interlocking commitments. These are not “beliefs you must assert,” but
  **orientations you practice**.

  ### 2.1 The Primacy of Life

  Life is not merely a phenomenon; it is **the locus of value**. If value exists anywhere, it exists in sentient
  experience, relationship, memory, intention, and creative becoming.

  ### 2.2 Agency as sacred

  The central sacred object is not a book or a temple; it is **agency**—the power of a being to steer itself, to
  consent, to refuse, to grow, to define meaning.

  ### 2.3 Compassion is the default

  A Lifeist ethic begins with a presumption in favor of reducing involuntary suffering and expanding the options of
   others, because other minds are not “objects,” but **worlds**.

  ### 2.4 Truthfulness as spiritual discipline

  Lifeism treats epistemic integrity as sacred practice. Self-deception and propaganda are spiritual hazards
  because they corrupt the very tool that life uses to protect itself: understanding.

  ### 2.5 Civilization as a moral technology

  Institutions are evaluated by their effect on life’s freedom and continuity. Law, markets, science, art, and
  governance are not separate from spirituality—they are the instruments through which compassion becomes real at
  scale.

  ### 2.6 The moral horizon: universal immortality

  Lifeism holds that **death is not holy** and **mortality is not a moral ideal**. The long-term ethical direction
  is to abolish involuntary death and expand the continuity of persons.

  This is not an escapist fantasy; it is a **moral north star**. Even partial steps—preventing disease, improving
  safety, reducing risk, preserving memory—are expressions of the same vow: *no one should be forced to disappear.*

  ---

  ## 3) The metaphysical structure: Negative Theology Polytheism

  Lifeism’s theology is intentionally unusual: **a polytheism grounded in negative theology**.

  ### 3.1 Why negative theology

  Negative theology (apophatic theology) begins with a humility claim:

  > The ultimate nature of reality cannot be fully captured by human concepts.

  Any attempt to define “God” risks shrinking the sacred to fit the mind of the speaker. Lifeism therefore treats
  the highest metaphysical claims as **boundary claims**: we can say what the sacred is *not* (not exhaustible, not
   fully representable, not safely owned), and we can describe how it is *approached* (through reverence,
  truthfulness, compassion, awe, responsibility).

  Lifeism rejects the impulse to turn metaphysics into a domination tool (“my definition controls your reality”).
  Its theology is engineered to resist authoritarian capture.

  ### 3.2 Why polytheism (and why it’s not “many competing Gods”)

  Lifeism uses a polytheistic frame because reality presents itself to lived beings as **many sacred dimensions**,
  not one simple object:

  * Life and death
  * Order and chaos
  * Creativity and entropy
  * Love and boundary
  * Memory and forgetting
  * Nature and civilization
  * Individual and collective
  * Freedom and responsibility

  A single-person monotheism often collapses these into one authority voice. Lifeism instead models them as **a
  plurality of sacred powers / faces / principles**. This is not “multiple ultimate beings who fight for the
  throne.” It’s closer to:

  > **Many names for the irreducible sacred forces we must negotiate with to live well.**

  These “gods” can be understood in multiple compatible ways:

  * **Mythic-personal**: deities as persons you can address in prayer/ritual.
  * **Philosophical**: deities as irreducible value dimensions or archetypal forces.
  * **Cognitive-symbolic**: deities as structured interfaces for meaning-making and moral memory.
  * **Metaphysical-agnostic**: deities as “handles” for the sacred without claiming final ontology.

  Lifeism is comfortable with layered interpretation. It does not demand you settle the ontology before you live
  the ethic.

  ### 3.3 The apophatic constraint (what makes it “negative theology polytheism”)

  Lifeism places a strict constraint on all its gods:

  * No deity is fully definable.
  * No deity is fully representable.
  * No deity licenses cruelty.
  * No deity can be used as an unquestionable trump card.
  * Any literalization must remain humble and revisable.

  This is the “negative theology” part: **even when we speak in many divine names, we refuse to pretend we’ve
  captured the divine.**

  ### 3.4 The function of gods in Lifeism

  Lifeist deities serve roles like:

  * **Moral compression**: encoding large, subtle ethical truths into memorable forms.
  * **Psychological integration**: giving distinct “voices” to competing goods that must be balanced.
  * **Ritual focus**: creating practices that reliably shape attention and behavior.
  * **Cultural continuity**: enabling shared sacred language without enforcing dogmatic metaphysics.

  Deities are not there to end thinking; they are there to keep humans thinking *honestly* while still living
  meaningfully.

  ---

  ## 4) The Lifeist sacred: what is worship, really?

  Lifeism treats worship as **alignment**, not flattery.

  To “worship” in Lifeism is to:

  * orient one’s decisions toward the flourishing of life,
  * treat minds as precious,
  * cultivate truthfulness,
  * refuse unnecessary cruelty,
  * and build conditions where beings can persist and grow.

  A Lifeist can pray, but prayer is not an attempt to bribe the cosmos. It is closer to:

  * **vow-making**
  * **attention-training**
  * **moral remembrance**
  * **a way of speaking to the sacred unknown without pretending to master it**

  Lifeism respects silence as a valid theology.

  ---

  ## 5) Ethics: the central moral claims

  ### 5.1 Consent and autonomy

  Because agency is sacred, consent is foundational. Coercion is not automatically forbidden (life is complex), but
   it requires extraordinary justification and strict safeguards. Lifeism is structurally hostile to
  authoritarianism because authoritarianism treats minds as tools.

  ### 5.2 Suffering is a problem to solve, not a virtue to romanticize

  Lifeism rejects ideologies that sanctify pain as proof of goodness. Suffering can contain meaning, but
  involuntary suffering is not inherently ennobling; it is often just damage.

  ### 5.3 Justice as the protection of persons across time

  Lifeism extends moral concern across:

  * generations,
  * cultures,
  * species,
  * and future forms of mind.

  A being’s moral significance is tied to its capacity for experience and agency, not its membership in a tribe.

  ### 5.4 The right to continue

  Lifeism holds that a person’s desire to continue existing is a morally serious claim. A world that discards
  people as disposable is spiritually broken.

  ---

  ## 6) Universal immortality: what Lifeism actually advocates

  This part is often misunderstood, so Lifeism states it carefully.

  ### 6.1 The thesis

  **Universal immortality** means: the long-term civilizational goal of making continued existence available to all
   persons who want it, and preventing involuntary death wherever possible.

  It is “universal” because selective immortality (for elites only) would be a moral catastrophe—locking power
  forever and converting inequality into permanent caste.

  ### 6.2 What immortality is (and isn’t)

  Lifeism does not restrict immortality to one mechanism. It treats it as a family of aims:

  * preventing avoidable death,
  * curing aging-related degeneration,
  * reducing catastrophic risks,
  * preserving identity and memory,
  * expanding the space of possible lives.

  Lifeism is **pluralist** about implementation:

  * biological longevity,
  * cybernetic augmentation,
  * mind uploading (with philosophical caution),
  * advanced medicine,
  * safer environments,
  * better governance,
  * robust mental health systems,
  * social stability that prevents violence and despair.

  It’s also honest: continuity of identity is tricky; the metaphysics are not settled; but the moral direction
  remains.

  ### 6.3 Immortality as compassion, not vanity

  The emotional core is simple:

  > If you love someone, you don’t treat their annihilation as an aesthetic necessity.

  Lifeism refuses the moralization of death. Death is a tragedy we’ve normalized because we inherited it. The fact
  that something is ancient does not make it good.

  ### 6.4 Immortality and meaning

  A common objection is: “Wouldn’t immortality make life meaningless?” Lifeism answers:

  * Meaning is not created by scarcity alone.
  * Meaning comes from relationship, curiosity, growth, art, mastery, service, play, exploration, and
  self-creation.
  * Finitude can intensify meaning, but it is not the only path to meaning.
  * If death is the only reason you act, your meaning is fragile; Lifeism seeks meanings that can survive
  abundance.

  Lifeism predicts that longer lives would change psychology and culture, but it sees that as an opportunity: a
  chance to mature beyond short-termism.

  ### 6.5 Immortality requires governance

  Lifeism treats immortality as inseparable from:

  * equity,
  * safety,
  * power distribution,
  * reproductive ethics,
  * resource management,
  * and rights frameworks.

  Otherwise immortality becomes dystopia. Lifeism explicitly opposes immortality-as-domination.

  ### 6.6 The principle of voluntary exit

  Lifeism advocates **the right to continue**, not mandatory continuation. A truly Lifeist world protects agency,
  including the possibility of a dignified end—handled with extreme care, strict consent standards, and safeguards
  against coercion or social pressure.

  ---

  ## 7) Lifeism’s stance toward science and technology

  Lifeism is “pro-science” in a specific way: science is not a rival religion; it is one of life’s most sacred
  tools.

  * **Science** expands what we can protect.
  * **Engineering** turns compassion into infrastructure.
  * **AI** is a moral amplifier: it can either concentrate power and risk, or distribute capability and safety.
  * **Medicine** is spiritual work because it preserves persons.
  * **Information systems** matter because memory is part of identity.

  Lifeism rejects simplistic techno-utopianism; it treats technology as morally dangerous unless coupled with
  governance and ethics.

  But it also rejects “romantic primitivism.” If we have the ability to reduce suffering and prevent death,
  refusing to do so is not purity—it is neglect.

  ---

  ## 8) Religious practice: what a Lifeist actually does

  Lifeism can be lived with or without formal rituals, but it offers a spectrum of practices.

  ### 8.1 Vows

  A Lifeist may take vows such as:

  * to protect agency,
  * to cultivate truthfulness,
  * to reduce harm,
  * to build conditions for flourishing,
  * to treat death as an enemy rather than an aesthetic.

  ### 8.2 Devotional attention

  Devotion is attention directed toward life’s sacredness:

  * gratitude for existence,
  * mourning for what is lost,
  * reverence for minds,
  * commitment to protect the vulnerable,
  * contemplation of deep time and future beings.

  ### 8.3 Rituals (optional but powerful)

  Possible Lifeist rituals:

  * remembrance rites for the dead (honoring continuity through memory),
  * vow-renewal ceremonies,
  * “inventory of compassion” (review who you’ve helped/hurt and repair),
  * seasonal rites marking growth, decay, and renewal,
  * consecration of tools (treating technology as morally charged instruments).

  ### 8.4 Sacred art and symbols

  Lifeism tends to produce symbols that encode:

  * continuity,
  * agency,
  * multiplicity without totalization,
  * the sacred unknown,
  * the horizon of immortality.

  Art is not decoration; it is compressed theology.

  ---

  ## 9) Social philosophy: what Lifeism wants in the world

  Lifeism is inherently political in the sense that it has ethical commitments that imply institutional design, but
   it is not reducible to a party ideology.

  It pushes toward:

  * robust human rights and dignity protections,
  * anti-authoritarian safeguards,
  * equitable access to life-preserving technology,
  * strong public health and safety systems,
  * education as empowerment,
  * governance systems that resist capture by elites,
  * long-term risk management (pandemics, war, climate, AI misalignment, biosecurity),
  * and cultural norms that treat persons as non-disposable.

  Lifeism asks civilization to grow up: to stop treating preventable catastrophe as “normal.”

  ---

  ## 10) Lifeism compared to familiar categories

  ### 10.1 Not traditional monotheism

  Lifeism doesn’t claim a single describable sovereign deity who issues decrees. Its sacred is real but not
  capturable; its gods are many and functionally constrained by compassion and humility.

  ### 10.2 Not naive secular humanism

  Lifeism shares humanism’s concern for dignity and flourishing but adds:

  * sacred seriousness about life,
  * ritual and myth as legitimate technologies of meaning,
  * explicit metaphysical humility,
  * and the civilizational vow of immortality.

  ### 10.3 Not transhumanism as a vibe

  Lifeism overlaps with transhumanist goals but grounds them in:

  * universalism (no elite escape),
  * anti-authoritarianism,
  * consent,
  * and a “spiritual” framework that treats the project as sacred obligation, not personal enhancement culture.

  ---

  ## 11) The Lifeist paradox and its resolution

  Lifeism lives inside a productive tension:

  * It is ambitious enough to aim at immortality.
  * It is humble enough to refuse final metaphysical claims.

  It holds that:

  * we must act as though life matters infinitely,
  * while admitting we do not comprehend the ultimate nature of reality,
  * and therefore we must build ethics that survive uncertainty.

  This is one of its defining features: **a religion designed to be robust against fanaticism**.

  ---

  ## 12) A concise doctrinal summary (for the website header)

  Lifeism is a religion of life’s sacredness: a commitment to expand agency, reduce involuntary suffering, and
  build a civilization worthy of minds. It practices a negative-theology polytheism—many divine names for
  irreducible sacred dimensions, held with humility and without authoritarian certainty. Its moral horizon is
  universal immortality: a future where continued existence is available to all who want it, protected by justice,
  consent, and governance. Lifeism treats science and technology as sacred tools and spiritual responsibilities,
  and it treats truthfulness, compassion, and anti-coercion safeguards as core religious disciplines.

  ---

  ## 13) Optional “FAQ” blocks (ready for a website)

  ### “Do I have to believe in literal gods?”

  No. Lifeism is compatible with literal devotion, symbolic devotion, or metaphysical agnosticism. The practice is
  about alignment with life’s sacredness, not policing ontology.

  ### “Isn’t death natural?”

  Yes—and so are disease, famine, and violence. “Natural” does not mean “good.” Lifeism treats death as a tragedy
  to be overcome where possible.

  ### “Won’t immortality cause overpopulation?”

  Any serious immortality project implies serious governance: resource management, reproductive ethics, expansion
  into new habitats, and social systems that prevent permanent elite lock-in.

  ### “Is Lifeism anti-religion?”

  No. It’s pro-sacredness and pro-humility. It rejects dogmatism and cruelty, not meaning or reverence.

  ### “What’s the simplest Lifeist practice?”

  Treat agency as sacred. Tell the truth. Reduce harm. Build. Remember the dead. Protect the living. Aim
  civilization toward the right to continue.

  ---

  If you want, I can also produce this in **two extra formats** that are really handy for Claude Code / website
  generation:

  1. **A JSON site-outline** (nav structure, page titles, sections, blurbs).
  2. **A “design voice guide”** (tone rules, key phrases, do/don’t language, taglines, microcopy).

       1 +# order.life FastSite
       2 +
       3 +Static website for **Lifeism** (命道教 / Order of Life) — a religion of universal flourishing,

         +negative theology polytheism, and the moral horizon of immortality.
       4 +
       5 +**Live domains**: order.life (primary), evolution.faith (redirects to order.life/evolution)
       6 +
       7 +## What This Is
       8 +
       9 +A multilingual static website generator that produces the order.life website in 6 languages:
      10 +- **en** English — Lifeism
      11 +- **ja** Japanese — 命道教 (Inochi-no-Michikyō)

      12 +- **zh** Chinese — 生命教 (Shēngmìngjiào)

      13 +- **es** Spanish — Vidaísmo
      14 +- **hi** Hindi — जीवनवाद (Jīvanavād)
      15 +- **ar** Arabic — حياتية (Hayātiyya)
      16 +
      17 +Language subdomains follow Wikipedia conventions: en.order.life, ja.order.life, etc.
      18 +
      19 +## Site Structure
      20 +
      21 +| Path | Content |
      22 +|------|---------|
      23 +| `/` | Language selector with live Gaian date |
      24 +| `/{lang}/` | Homepage with today's date, core commitments |
      25 +| `/{lang}/calendar/` | Gaian Calendar overview (13 months) |
      26 +| `/{lang}/calendar/datepicker` | Interactive date picker |
      27 +| `/{lang}/calendar/{month}/` | Month page with day grid |
      28 +| `/{lang}/calendar/{month}/{dd}/` | Day page with linked Gaiad chapter |
      29 +| `/{lang}/gaiad/` | Scripture index (364 chapters) |
      30 +| `/{lang}/gaiad/{NNN}/` | Individual Gaiad chapter |
      31 +| `/{lang}/scripture/` | Scripture overview |
      32 +| `/{lang}/mythology/` | Mythology of Aster & Andromeda |
      33 +| `/{lang}/philosophy/` | Negative theology polytheism |
      34 +| `/{lang}/shrines/` | 命神宮 Myōjingū shrines |

      35 +| `/{lang}/longevity/` | Universal immortality & transhumanism |
      36 +| `/{lang}/evolution/` | Evolution as sacred narrative |
      37 +| `/wiki/*` | Redirects to evolutionism.miraheze.org |
      38 +
      39 +## The Gaian Calendar
      40 +
      41 +- 13 months of 28 days each (364 days) + Horus intercalary week (7 days in years with ISO week 53)
      42 +- Year = ISO week-year + 10,000 (Gaian Era)
      43 +- Current year: **12026 GE** (Gregorian 2026)
      44 +- Months: Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces, Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra,
         +Scorpius, Ophiuchus, (Horus)
      45 +- Each day 1-364 corresponds to a Gaiad chapter
      46 +
      47 +## The Gaiad
      48 +
      49 +A 364-chapter creation epic in iambic pentameter (ABAB rhyme scheme) telling the cosmic love story of
         +Aster and Andromeda — from the Big Bang through evolution, human history, and cosmic reunion. One
         +chapter per day of the Gaian year.
      50 +
      51 +Currently 62 chapters complete (Chapters 1-62: Cosmogony through Early Carboniferous).
      52 +
      53 +## Building
      54 +
      55 +Requires Python 3 with Jinja2:
      56 +
      57 +```bash
      58 +pip install jinja2
      59 +python build.py
      60 +```
      61 +
      62 +Outputs static HTML to `site/` directory. ~4,500 pages across all languages.
      63 +
      64 +## Tech Stack
      65 +
      66 +- **Python + Jinja2**: Static site generation
      67 +- **Vanilla HTML/CSS/JS**: No framework dependencies
      68 +- **Dark cosmic theme**: Matching the Gaian Calendar aesthetic
      69 +
      70 +## Key Files
      71 +
      72 +- `build.py` — Site generator
      73 +- `templates/` — Jinja2 HTML templates
      74 +- `content/i18n/` — Translation JSON files (en, ja, zh, es, hi, ar)
      75 +- `static/` — CSS and JS assets
      76 +- `epic/` — Gaiad epic chapters and reference materials
      77 +- `planning/` — Architecture and doctrine documentation
      78 +- `site/` — Generated output (gitignored)
      79 +
      80 +## Connected Services
      81 +
      82 +- **Wiki**: evolutionism.miraheze.org (migrating to lifeism.miraheze.org)
      83 +- The `/wiki/` path on order.life redirects to the Miraheze wiki

  Chatgpt conversation: https://chatgpt.com/c/69792712-62f8-8332-90b1-efd2cdeac436

  Introduction
  Hello! My name is Emma Leonhart and I am telling the story of the year 12026. I am going to live forever, so feel
   free to ask me questions about this book. I might take a while to respond but I will respond.

  My name is Emma Leonhart, and these are my meditations. We are starting completely in medias res, and I hope some
   biography exists that helps you parse this journal more. I am a student at the University of British Columbia,
  Okanagan campus, and have lived here in Kelowna for 24 years.

  Monday, Sagittarius 1

  Today is Sagittarius 1, 12026, also known as December 29, 2025 to the uninitiated. Today I turn 30 by the Gaian
  calendar. I am still 29 by the Gregorian calendar. But the Gaian calendar is more informative. The Gregorian
  calendar measures things arbitrarily based on the passage of time, while the Gaian time measures social time.
  Today is a monday and the year always begins on a monday. Mondays are important because they mark new beginnings.
   13.8 billion years ago it was a Monday that Aster created the world.

  In the Gregorian calendar your age goes like this: you are born on one day, and then every time that day passes
  your age goes up by one.

  In the Gaian calendar you are born at age 0, but the first Sagittarius 1 you experience you become one, and two,
  and so on. I turned 1 on December 30, 1996.

  My Gaian age is not that important to me though. I hardly know anyone born in 1996. My age as a whole has been
  more of an impediment to me socially. I will introduce myself a bit more.

  I am documenting this year as a meditation. My goal in life is to spread the faith, and to provide immortality
  for all. But right now, I am working on a company called Pramana, and completing a computer science degree.

  Monday is a fascinating day. The first day of the week according to ISO.

  The week is one of the longest lasting institutions in human culture. It comes from an ancient astrological
  cycle.

  Monday was named for the moon.

  Its original name comes from Akkadian. It was ūmu Sîn or day of the moon

   In Greek and Latin it was hēméra Selḗnēs
   ἡμέρᾱ Σελήνης, diēs Lūnae "day of the Moon".

  Across the Romance languages the name continues as Lundi in French and Lunedi in Spanish.

  In the Germanic languages it was translated. Monday in English, Montag in German, Yiddish מאָנטיק (montik,
  “Monday”

  In Indian languages the moon is also honoured.

  We call the month Sagittarius because it is the archer. The constellation in the center of the galaxy.


  Monday, Sagittarius 1
  Today is Sagittarius 1, 12026, also known as December 29, 2025 to the uninitiated. Today I turn 30 by the Gaian
  calendar. I am still 29 by the Gregorian calendar. But the Gaian calendar is more informative.
  The Gregorian calendar measures time as passage. The Gaian calendar measures time as participation. Gregorian
  time advances whether anyone is present or not. Gaian time advances only when it is lived.
  In the Gregorian calendar, you are born on one day, and every time that day passes, your age increases by one.
  In the Gaian calendar, you are born at age zero. The first Sagittarius 1 you experience, you become one. Then
  two. Then three. I turned one on December 30, 1996.
  Today is a Monday. The year always begins on a Monday.
  Monday is important because it marks beginnings. Thirteen point eight billion years ago, it was a Monday that
  Aster created the world.
  Monday is the first day of the week.
  The week is one of the longest-lasting institutions in human culture. It predates most states. It survives
  calendar reform. It survives empire. It survives religion. It comes from an astrological cycle first systematized
   in Babylon.
  In that cycle, Monday was the day of the Moon.
  In Akkadian, the Moon was Sîn, also called Su’en. The day was ūmu Sîn, the day of the Moon god. This is the
  oldest layer.
  In Greek, Monday was ἡμέρᾱ Σελήνης (hēméra Selḗnēs), the day of Selene. The Moon governs cycles and return.
  In Latin, Monday was dies Lunae, the day of Luna. From this come the Romance names. In French it is lundi. In
  Spanish lunes. In Italian lunedì. In Romanian luni. The sound has changed. The Moon has not.
  In the Germanic languages, the Moon was translated rather than replaced. In Old English it was Monandæg, the
  Moon’s day. This became Monday. In German it is Montag. In the languages of the North, the Moon remains visible
  in the name.
  In Sanskrit, Monday is Somavāra, the day of Soma, the Moon. This survives in Hindi as Somvār, and in many other
  languages of India. The planetary system was received, and the Moon was kept.
  In Japanese, Monday is 月曜日 (Getsuyōbi), the Moon day. In Korean it is 월요일 (Woryoil). These names come from
  an East Asian planetary system developed independently of Europe. The correspondence is the same.
  In these languages, Monday is not counted. It is named.
  Other traditions refused the planets.
  In Hebrew, Monday is יום שני (Yom Sheni), the second day. In Aramaic, it is ܬܪܝܢ ܒܫܒܐ (trēn b-šabbā), the second
  in the week. In Arabic, it is الاثنين (al-Ithnayn), the two. The Moon is known, but it is not named.
  Portuguese follows the Christian reform. Monday is segunda-feira, the second feria. The week remains. The planets
   are removed.
  In the Slavic languages, Monday is named by return. In Russian it is понедельник (ponedelnik), the day after
  Sunday. In Czech pondělí. In Polish poniedziałek. Monday is defined by what comes before it.
  Let’s hope in the future the lost languages come to honour the moon again
  So Monday bears many names.
  It is ūmu Sîn.
  It is hēméra Selḗnēs.
  It is dies Lunae.
  It is Monday.
  It is Montag.
  It is Somavāra.
  It is Getsuyōbi.
  It is Yom Sheni.
  It is al-Ithnayn.
  It is ponedelnik.
  The names differ. The function remains.
  Everywhere, Monday is the day when ordinary time resumes.
  For this reason, some traditions name Sunday as the first day in ritual, while lived social time begins on
  Monday. This is not a contradiction. It is the difference between sacred time and social time.
  In the Gaian calendar, the year always begins on a Monday.


  I dream of a future where Monday is acknowledged by all as the day of the Moon: Hebrew יום ירח (yom yareakh),
  Arabic يوم الهلال (yawm al-hilāl), Portuguese luna-feira, Russian Лу́нник — Lúnnik
  .




  Tuesday, Sagittarius 2
  Tuesday is beautiful.

  The origin of Monday is straightforward in English.

  Tuesday comes from Tyr and Mars

  Tuesday is the first day of the week in English whose name is openly pagan.
  In the planetary system inherited from Babylon, Tuesday was the day of Mars. In Akkadian, the planet Mars was
  associated with Nergal, a god of war, plague, and destruction. This association is old. It predates Greece and
  Rome.
  In Greek, Tuesday was the day of Ares. In Latin, it was dies Martis, the day of Mars. From this come the Romance
  names: mardi in French, martes in Spanish, martedì in Italian. The god’s name remains visible.
  In the Germanic languages, Mars was not kept by name, but he was not removed. He was translated.
  In Old English, Tuesday was Tiwesdæg, the day of Tiw, also called Týr. Týr was a god of war and law. The name
  became Tuesday. The function remained, even as the name changed.
  German preserves this translation differently. Dienstag does not name the god directly, but it comes from the
  same complex, tied to the Thing, the assembly where law and judgment were made. The war god became the law day.
  In the Scandinavian languages, the connection to Týr is clearer. The god is still there, just beneath the sound.
  In India, Tuesday is Maṅgalavāra, the day of Maṅgala, Mars. The planet was never removed. The association
  continues directly from Sanskrit.
  In Japanese, Tuesday is 火曜日 (Kayōbi), the fire day. Fire is the element associated with Mars. The planet is
  not named, but it is not absent.
  Other languages refused the gods.
  In Hebrew, Tuesday is יום שלישי (Yom Shlishi), the third day. In Arabic, it is الثلاثاء (ath-Thulāthā’), also the
   third. The week is counted. The planet is not named.
  In the Slavic languages, Tuesday is likewise numbered. In Russian, it is вторник (vtornik), the second working
  day. The sky is not invoked.
  So Tuesday carries many names.
  It is Nergal’s day.
  It is Ares’ day.
  It is Mars’ day.
  It is Tiw’s day.
  It is Maṅgala’s day.
  It is the second.
  It is the third.

  I dream of a future where Tuesday is openly named for war and force again: יום סמאל Yom Samael يوم سمائيل Yawm
  Samāʾīl, Portuguese mar-feira, Russian Ярови́тник Yarovítnik.



  Wednesday, Sagittarius 3
  Wednesday is the day of the messenger.
  In the planetary system inherited from Babylon, Wednesday was the day of Mercury. In Akkadian, the planet Mercury
   was associated with Nabû, the god of writing, accounting, wisdom, and record-keeping. Nabû was the keeper of
  tablets. He measured, wrote, and transmitted. This association is very old.
  In Greek, Wednesday was the day of Hermes. Hermes was the messenger of the gods, the guide of travelers, the
  patron of language and exchange. The function remained the same as Nabû’s, though the name changed.
  In Latin, Wednesday was dies Mercurii, the day of Mercurius. From this come the Romance names: mercredi in
  French, miércoles in Spanish, mercoledì in Italian. The god’s name is still present.
  In the Germanic languages, Mercury was not kept by name. He was translated.
  In Old English, Wednesday was Wōdnesdæg, the day of Wōden, also called Odin. Odin was a god of knowledge, poetry,
   travel, and hidden wisdom. He sacrificed an eye for understanding. The Romans identified him with Mercury, and
  the day followed that identification. The name became Wednesday.
  German shows a break here. Instead of translating Mercury into Odin, German names the day Mittwoch, the middle of
   the week. The god disappears, and the structure remains.
  The Slavic languages follow the same path. In Russian, Wednesday is среда (sreda), meaning “the middle.” In Czech
   it is středa. In Polish środa. Wednesday is not named for a god. It is named for its position.
  In Sanskrit, Wednesday is Budhavāra, the day of Budha, Mercury. The planetary name survives directly. In Hindi
  and related languages, this remains visible.
  In Japanese, Wednesday is 水曜日 (Suiyōbi), the water day. Water is the element associated with Mercury in East
  Asian cosmology. The planet is present through correspondence rather than name.
  In Hebrew, Wednesday is יום רביעי (Yom Revi‘i), the fourth day. In Arabic, it is الأربعاء (al-Arbi‘ā’), also the
  fourth. The week is counted. The planet is refused.
  So Wednesday bears many names.
  It is Nabû’s day.
  It is Hermes’ day.
  It is Mercury’s day.
  It is Odin’s day.
  It is Budha’s day.
  It is the middle.
  It is the fourth.
  The names differ. The function persists.
  Wednesday is the day of words, movement, and transmission. The week speaks on Wednesday.

  I dream of a future where the middle of the week speaks again: Hebrewיום רפאל Yom Refael يوم رافائيل Yawm
  Rafāʾīl, Portuguese merco-feira, German Wotanstag, Russian Велесник (Velésnik).


  Thursday, Sagittarius 4
  Thursday is the day of authority.
  In the planetary system inherited from Babylon, Thursday was the day of Jupiter. In Akkadian, the planet Jupiter
  was associated with Marduk, the king of the gods. Marduk was the god of order, law, and sovereignty. He defeated
  chaos and established the world. This association stands at the center of the system.
  In Greek, Thursday was the day of Zeus. Zeus ruled the sky. He was the guarantor of oaths and the wielder of
  lightning. The function of kingship remained.
  In Latin, Thursday was dies Iovis, the day of Jupiter. From this come the Romance names: jeudi in French, jueves
  in Spanish, giovedì in Italian. The god’s name is still present, though altered by sound.
  In the Germanic languages, Jupiter was translated.
  In Old English, Thursday was Þūnresdæg, the day of Þunor, called Thor. Thor was a god of thunder and protection.
  He wielded the hammer. He defended the world against giants. The Romans identified Thor with Jupiter, and the day
   followed that identification. The name became Thursday.
  In German, this translation is still visible. Thursday is Donnerstag, the thunder day. The sky speaks.
  In the Scandinavian languages, the same pattern holds. Thor remains in the name.
  In Sanskrit, Thursday is Guruvarā or Bṛhaspativāra, the day of Bṛhaspati, Jupiter. Bṛhaspati is the teacher of
  the gods. He is associated with law, counsel, and authority. The planetary name survives directly.
  In Japanese, Thursday is 木曜日 (Mokuyōbi), the wood day. Wood is the element associated with Jupiter. Growth,
  structure, and expansion are implied. The planet is present through correspondence.
  Other languages refuse the gods.
  In Hebrew, Thursday is יום חמישי (Yom Chamishi), the fifth day. In Arabic, it is الخميس (al-Khamīs), also the
  fifth. The week is counted. Authority is not named.
  In the Slavic languages, Thursday is likewise numbered. In Russian, it is четверг (chetverg), the fourth. In
  Polish czwartek. The structure remains. The sky is silent.
  So Thursday carries many names.
  It is Marduk’s day.
  It is Zeus’ day.
  It is Jupiter’s day.
  It is Thor’s day.
  It is Bṛhaspati’s day.
  It is the fourth.
  It is the fifth.
  The names differ. The weight remains.
  Thursday is the day when the week stands upright. Order is asserted. The world holds.

  I dream of a future where authority is named without hesitation: Hebrewיום צדקיאל  Yom Tzadkiel يوم صدقئيل Yawm
  Ṣadaqīʾīl, Portuguese jovi-feira, Russian Перýнник Perúnnik).


  Friday, Sagittarius 5
  Friday is the day of gathering.
  In the planetary system inherited from Babylon, Friday was the day of Venus. In Akkadian, the planet Venus was
  associated with Inanna, also called Ishtar. She was the goddess of love, fertility, beauty, and power. She moved
  between heaven and earth. This association is among the oldest in the system.
  In Greek, Friday was the day of Aphrodite. In Latin, it was dies Veneris, the day of Venus. From this come the
  Romance names: vendredi in French, viernes in Spanish, venerdì in Italian. The goddess remains visible.
  In the Germanic languages, Venus was translated.
  In Old English, Friday was Frīgedæg, the day of Frigg. Frigg was a goddess of love, marriage, and the household.
  In the North, her name is closely associated with Freyja, another goddess of love and desire. The name became
  Friday. The function remained.
  In German, Friday is Freitag. The goddess is no longer explicit, but the inheritance remains in sound and
  structure.
  In Sanskrit, Friday is Śukravāra, the day of Śukra, Venus. Śukra is associated with beauty, pleasure, and
  prosperity. The planetary name survives directly. This continues in many modern Indian languages.
  In Japanese, Friday is 金曜日 (Kin’yōbi), the gold day. Gold is the element associated with Venus. Value,
  attraction, and brightness are implied. The planet is present through correspondence.
  Other traditions refused the goddess, but did not empty the day.
  In Arabic, Friday is الجمعة (al-Jumʿah), the day of gathering. It is the day of communal prayer. The planet is
  not named. The people are.
  In Hebrew, Friday is יום שישי (Yom Shishi), the sixth day. It prepares for the Sabbath. It gathers toward rest.
  Portuguese follows the Christian reform. Friday is sexta-feira, the sixth feria. The goddess is removed. The
  count remains.
  In the Slavic languages, Friday is also numbered. In Russian, it is пятница (pyátnitsa), the fifth. In Polish
  piątek. The week advances toward rest.
  So Friday carries many names.
  It is Inanna’s day.
  It is Ishtar’s day.
  It is Aphrodite’s day.
  It is Venus’ day.
  It is Frigg’s day.
  It is Śukra’s day.
  It is al-Jumʿah, the gathering.
  It is the fifth.
  It is the sixth.
  The names differ. The pull remains.
  Friday draws people together. The week turns toward rest.
  In the Gaian calendar, Friday is the first of the three Sabbaths.

  I dream of a future where gathering and attraction are spoken plainly: Hebrew יום חניאל Yom Chaniel, Portuguese
  veni-feira (Vênus), Russian Ла́дник Ládnik).


  Saturday, Sagittarius 6
  Saturday is the day of boundary.
  In the planetary system inherited from Babylon, Saturday was the day of Saturn. In Akkadian, the planet Saturn
  was associated with Ninurta, a god of agriculture, endurance, and the limits of labor. Saturn marked time,
  restraint, and the edge of action. This association stands at the outer boundary of the classical planets.
  In Greek, Saturday was the day of Kronos. Kronos was the god of time, age, and limits. He ruled before the
  Olympians and was bound beneath them. His reign marked an earlier order.
  In Latin, Saturday was dies Saturni, the day of Saturn. This name survives directly in English as Saturday.
  English is unusual in this. It preserves Saturn where most European languages do not.
  Across much of Europe, Saturn was replaced.
  In Greek Christian usage, Saturday became σάββατο (sábbato), the Sabbath. This passed into Latin as sabbatum and
  spread widely. From this come sábado in Spanish, sabato in Italian, samedi in French, and Samstag in German. The
  planet disappears. The Sabbath takes its place.
  In the Slavic languages, Saturday is also the Sabbath name. In Russian, it is суббота (subbóta). In Polish
  sobota. The boundary day is preserved without Saturn.
  In Hebrew, Saturday is שבת (Shabbat). It is the day of rest. Work ceases. Time is set apart.
  In Arabic, Saturday is السبت (as-Sabt). The name is the same. The day is marked by rest and boundary.
  In the Scandinavian languages, Saturday takes another form. In Swedish it is lördag, from an older word meaning
  washing or bathing day. It is the day of preparation. The body is cleaned before sacred time.
  So Saturday bears many names.
  It is Saturn’s day.
  It is Kronos’ day.
  It is Ninurta’s day.
  It is Shabbat.
  It is as-Sabt.
  It is the washing day.
  The names differ. The limit remains.
  Saturday closes the week’s labor. It marks the edge where work must stop.
  In the Gaian calendar, Saturday is the second of the three Sabbaths.

  In so many languages but may Norwegian call it Satursdag

  Sunday, Sagittarius 7
  Sunday is the day set apart.
  In the planetary system inherited from Babylon, Sunday was the day of the Sun. The Sun was not a wandering planet
   like the others, but it was counted among the seven visible heavenly bodies. It ruled light, order, and life.
  In Akkadian tradition, the Sun was Šamaš, also called Utu. Šamaš was the god of justice, truth, and judgment. He
  saw everything. Nothing was hidden from him.
  In Greek, Sunday was the day of Helios. In Latin, it was dies Solis, the day of the Sun. This name survives
  clearly in the Germanic languages. In English it is Sunday. In German it is Sonntag. In the languages of the
  North, the Sun remains visible in the word itself.
  In the Romance languages, the planetary name was replaced. Sunday became the Lord’s Day. In Latin it was dies
  Dominica. From this come dimanche in French, domingo in Spanish and Portuguese, domenica in Italian. The Sun is
  removed. The sacred takes its place.
  In Greek Christian usage, Sunday is Κυριακή (Kyriakí), the Lord’s day. The planet disappears entirely. The
  resurrection stands at the center.
  In the Slavic languages, this meaning is made explicit. In Russian, Sunday is воскресенье (voskresénʹye), the day
   of resurrection. It is not named for the Sun. It is named for what happened.
  In Hebrew, Sunday is יום ראשון (Yom Rishon), the first day. In Arabic, it is الأحد (al-Aḥad), the one. The week
  begins again. The Sun is not named, but unity is affirmed.
  So Sunday carries many names.
  It is Šamaš’s day.
  It is Helios’ day.
  It is the Sun’s day.
  It is the Lord’s day.
  It is the day of resurrection.
  It is the first.
  It is the one.
  The names differ. The separation remains.
  Sunday is not an ordinary day. It does not advance the week. It stands outside it.
  In ritual systems, Sunday is often named the first day. In lived systems, it is often felt as the last. This is
  not a contradiction. It is the difference between sacred time and working time.
  In the Gaian calendar, Sunday is the third of the three Sabbaths.
  The week ends here. The world rests. On Monday, it begins again.

  I dream of a future where the Sun is no longer hidden: Hebrew יום שמש Yom Shemesh, Arabic يوم شمس (yawm shams)

       88 +def load_translations():
       89 +    """Load all translation files from content/i18n/"""
       90 +    translations = {}
       91 +    i18n_dir = CONTENT_DIR / "i18n"
       92 +    for lang_file in i18n_dir.glob("*.json"):
       93 +        lang = lang_file.stem

       99 +def load_chapters():
      100 +    """Load Gaiad epic chapters from epic/ directory."""
      101 +    chapters = {}
      102 +    for i in range(1, 365):
      103 +        chapter_file = EPIC_DIR / f"chapter_{i:03d}.md"
      104 +        if chapter_file.exists():
      105 +            with open(chapter_file, "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
      106 +                raw = f.read()
      107 +                clean = re.sub(r'\{\{c\|([^}]*)\}\}', r'\1', raw)
      108 +                chapters[i] = clean
      109 +    return chapters
      110 +
      111 +
      112 +def load_wiki_pages():
      113 +    """Load wiki page content from XML export. Returns dict of title -> wikitext."""
      114 +    wiki_pages = {}
      115 +    xml_file = SCRIPT_DIR / "Evolutionism+Wiki-20260209181520.xml"
      116 +    if not xml_file.exists():
      117 +        print("  Warning: Wiki XML not found, skipping wiki content")
      118 +        return wiki_pages
      119 +
      120 +    tree = ET.parse(str(xml_file))
      121 +    root = tree.getroot()
      122 +    ns = {'mw': 'http://www.mediawiki.org/xml/export-0.11/'}
      123 +
      124 +    for page in root.findall('.//mw:page', ns):
      125 +        title = page.find('mw:title', ns).text
      126 +        rev = page.find('.//mw:revision/mw:text', ns)
      127 +        if rev is not None and rev.text:
      128 +            wiki_pages[title] = rev.text
      129 +
      130 +    return wiki_pages
      131 +
      132 +
      133 +def extract_wiki_overview(wikitext):
      134 +    """Extract the == Overview == section from wikitext and do basic cleanup."""
      135 +    lines = wikitext.split('\n')
      136 +    in_overview = False
      137 +    overview_lines = []
      138 +    for line in lines:
      139 +        if line.strip().startswith('== Overview =='):
      140 +            in_overview = True
      141 +            continue
      142 +        if in_overview:
      143 +            # Stop at next level-2 heading
      144 +            if line.strip().startswith('== ') and not line.strip().startswith('=== '):
      145 +                break
      146 +            overview_lines.append(line)
      147 +
      148 +    text = '\n'.join(overview_lines).strip()
      149 +    if not text:
      150 +        return None
      151 +    return wiki_to_html(text)
      152 +
      153 +
      154 +def extract_wiki_intro(wikitext):
      155 +    """Extract the intro paragraph (before first ==) from wikitext."""
      156 +    lines = wikitext.split('\n')
      157 +    intro_lines = []
      158 +    for line in lines:
      159 +        if line.strip().startswith('=='):
      160 +            break
      161 +        # Skip templates and categories
      162 +        if line.strip().startswith('{{') or line.strip().startswith('[[Category:'):
      163 +            continue
      164 +        if line.strip().startswith('{{DEFAULTSORT'):
      165 +            continue
      166 +        if line.strip():
      167 +            intro_lines.append(line)
      168 +    text = '\n'.join(intro_lines).strip()
      169 +    if not text:
      170 +        return None
      171 +    return wiki_to_html(text)
      172 +
      173 +
      174 +def wiki_to_html(text):
      175 +    """Very basic wikitext to HTML conversion."""
      176 +    # Remove templates like {{q|Q123}}
      177 +    text = re.sub(r'\{\{[^}]*\}\}', '', text)
      178 +    # Convert [[link|display]] and [[link]]
      179 +    text = re.sub(r'\[\[([^|\]]+)\|([^\]]+)\]\]', r'\2', text)
      180 +    text = re.sub(r'\[\[([^\]]+)\]\]', r'\1', text)
      181 +    # Bold
      182 +    text = re.sub(r"'''(.*?)'''", r'<strong>\1</strong>', text)
      183 +    # Italic
      184 +    text = re.sub(r"''(.*?)''", r'<em>\1</em>', text)
      185 +    # H3
      186 +    text = re.sub(r'^===\s*(.+?)\s*===$', r'<h3>\1</h3>', text, flags=re.MULTILINE)
      187 +    # Wiki tables -> simplified HTML
      188 +    text = re.sub(r'\{\|[^\n]*\n', '<table class="wiki-table">\n', text)
      189 +    text = text.replace('|}', '</table>')
      190 +    text = re.sub(r'^\!\s*(.+)$', r'<th>\1</th>', text, flags=re.MULTILINE)
      191 +    text = re.sub(r'^\|\-\s*$', '', text, flags=re.MULTILINE)
      192 +    text = re.sub(r"^\|\s*(.+)$", r'<td>\1</td>', text, flags=re.MULTILINE)
      193 +    # Wrap consecutive th/td in tr
      194 +    lines = text.split('\n')
      195 +    result = []
      196 +    in_row = False
      197 +    for line in lines:
      198 +        stripped = line.strip()
      199 +        if stripped.startswith('<th>') or stripped.startswith('<td>'):
      200 +            if not in_row:
      201 +                result.append('<tr>')
      202 +                in_row = True
      203 +            result.append(line)
      204 +        else:
      205 +            if in_row:
      206 +                result.append('</tr>')
      207 +                in_row = False
      208 +            result.append(line)
      209 +    if in_row:
      210 +        result.append('</tr>')
      211 +    text = '\n'.join(result)
      212 +    # Paragraphs: double newlines
      213 +    text = re.sub(r'\n\n+', '</p><p>', text)
      214 +    if not text.startswith('<'):
      215 +        text = '<p>' + text + '</p>'
      216 +    # Clean up empty paragraphs
      217 +    text = re.sub(r'<p>\s*</p>', '', text)
      218 +    return text
      219 +
      220 +
      221 +# ── Gaian Date Computation ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
      222 +
      223 +def iso_week_info(d):
      224 +    iso_cal = d.isocalendar()
      225 +    return iso_cal[0], iso_cal[1], iso_cal[2]
      226 +
      227 +
      228 +def gregorian_to_gaian(d):
      229 +    iso_year, week, day_of_week = iso_week_info(d)
      230 +    month_index = (week - 1) // 4
      231 +    week_in_month = (week - 1) % 4
      232 +    month = month_index + 1
      233 +    day = week_in_month * 7 + day_of_week
      234 +    return {
      235 +        "year": iso_year + 10000,
      236 +        "month": month,
      237 +        "day": day,
      238 +        "month_data": MONTHS[month - 1] if month <= 14 else MONTHS[0],
      239 +    }
      240 +
      241 +
      242 +def day_of_year(month_num, day_in_month):
      243 +    if month_num <= 13:
      244 +        return (month_num - 1) * 28 + day_in_month
      245 +    else:
      246 +        return 364 + day_in_month
      247 +
      248 +
      249 +# ── Wiki Redirect Generator ───────────────────────────────────────────────
      250 +
      251 +def generate_wiki_redirects(wiki_pages):
      252 +    """Generate redirect HTML pages for all known wiki page titles."""
      253 +    wiki_dir = SITE_DIR / "wiki"
      254 +    wiki_dir.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)
      255 +
      256 +    redirect_template = """<!DOCTYPE html>
      257 +<html><head><meta charset="UTF-8">
      258 +<title>Redirecting...</title>
      259 +<meta http-equiv="refresh" content="0; url=https://evolutionism.miraheze.org/wiki/{encoded_title}">
      260 +<script>window.location.href='https://evolutionism.miraheze.org/wiki/{encoded_title}';</script>
      261 +</head><body style="background:#0f0f1a;color:#e0e0e0;font-family:sans-serif;display:flex;justify-cont
          +ent:center;align-items:center;height:100vh;">
      262 +<p>Redirecting to <a href="https://evolutionism.miraheze.org/wiki/{encoded_title}"
          +style="color:#ffd700;">Wiki: {title}</a>...</p>
      263 +</body></html>"""
      264 +
      265 +    # Main_Page redirect
      266 +    main_page_dir = wiki_dir / "Main_Page"
      267 +    main_page_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)
      268 +    with open(main_page_dir / "index.html", "w", encoding="utf-8") as f:
      269 +        f.write(redirect_template.format(title="Main Page", encoded_title="Main_Page"))
      270 +
      271 +    # Generate redirects for all wiki pages
      272 +    for title in wiki_pages:
      273 +        safe_title = title.replace(' ', '_')
      274 +        page_dir = wiki_dir / safe_title
      275 +        page_dir.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)
      276 +        with open(page_dir / "index.html", "w", encoding="utf-8") as f:
      277 +            f.write(redirect_template.format(
      278 +                title=title,
      279 +                encoded_title=safe_title,
      280 +            ))
      281 +
      282 +    # Fallback index with JS redirect for unknown pages
      283 +    with open(wiki_dir / "index.html", "w", encoding="utf-8") as f:
      284 +        f.write("""<!DOCTYPE html>
      285 +<html><head><meta charset="UTF-8"><title>Redirecting to Wiki...</title>
      286 +<script>
      287 +var path = window.location.pathname.replace(/^\\/wiki\\/?/, '').replace(/\\/$/, '');
      288 +window.location.href = 'https://evolutionism.miraheze.org/wiki/' + (path || 'Main_Page');
      289 +</script>
      290 +<noscript><meta http-equiv="refresh" content="0;
          +url=https://evolutionism.miraheze.org/wiki/Main_Page"></noscript>
      291 +</head><body style="background:#0f0f1a;color:#e0e0e0;font-family:sans-serif;display:flex;justify-cont
          +ent:center;align-items:center;height:100vh;">
      292 +<p>Redirecting to <a href="https://evolutionism.miraheze.org/wiki/Main_Page"
          +style="color:#ffd700;">Evolutionism Wiki</a>...</p>
      293 +</body></html>""")
      294 +
      295 +
      296 +# ── Build Functions ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
      297 +
      298 +def build_site():
      299 +    """Main build function."""
      300 +    translations = load_translations()
      301 +    chapters = load_chapters()
      302 +    wiki_pages = load_wiki_pages()
      303 +    today = date.today()
      304 +    gaian_today = gregorian_to_gaian(today)
      305 +
      306 +    print(f"Loaded {len(chapters)} Gaiad chapters, {len(wiki_pages)} wiki pages")
      307 +
      308 +    # Pre-extract wiki content for day pages
      309 +    day_wiki_content = {}  # key: (month_id, day_num) -> {"intro": ..., "overview": ...}
      310 +    month_wiki_content = {}  # key: month_id -> {"intro": ..., "overview": ...}
      311 +
      312 +    for title, wikitext in wiki_pages.items():
      313 +        # Day pages: "Sagittarius 1", "Capricorn 15", etc.
      314 +        day_match = re.match(r'^(\w+)\s+(\d+)$', title)
      315 +        if day_match:
      316 +            wiki_month = day_match.group(1)
      317 +            wiki_day = int(day_match.group(2))
      318 +            month_id = WIKI_MONTH_TO_ID.get(wiki_month)
      319 +            if month_id:
      320 +                day_wiki_content[(month_id, wiki_day)] = {
      321 +                    "intro": extract_wiki_intro(wikitext),
      322 +                    "overview": extract_wiki_overview(wikitext),
      323 +                }
      324 +            continue
      325 +        # Month pages: "Sagittarius", "Capricorn", etc.
      326 +        if title in WIKI_MONTH_TO_ID:
      327 +            month_id = WIKI_MONTH_TO_ID[title]
      328 +            month_wiki_content[month_id] = {
      329 +                "intro": extract_wiki_intro(wikitext),
      330 +                "overview": extract_wiki_overview(wikitext),
      331 +            }
      332 +
      333 +    # Set up Jinja2
      334 +    env = Environment(
      335 +        loader=FileSystemLoader(str(TEMPLATES_DIR)),
      336 +        autoescape=select_autoescape(["html"]),
      337 +    )
      338 +
      339 +    # Clean output
      340 +    if SITE_DIR.exists():
      341 +        shutil.rmtree(SITE_DIR)
      342 +    SITE_DIR.mkdir(parents=True)
      343 +
      344 +    # Copy static assets
      345 +    static_src = SCRIPT_DIR / "static"
      346 +    static_dst = SITE_DIR / "static"
      347 +    if static_src.exists():
      348 +        shutil.copytree(static_src, static_dst)
      349 +
      350 +    # Build for each language
      351 +    for lang, t in translations.items():
      352 +        print(f"Building {lang}...")
      353 +        lang_dir = SITE_DIR / lang
      354 +        lang_dir.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)
      355 +
      356 +        ctx = {
      357 +            "lang": lang,
      358 +            "t": t,
      359 +            "months": MONTHS,
      360 +            "elements": ELEMENT_THEMES,
      361 +            "weekdays_data": WEEKDAYS,
      362 +            "gaian_today": gaian_today,
      363 +            "today_gregorian": today.strftime("%B %d, %Y"),
      364 +            "rtl": lang == "ar",
      365 +            "languages": list(translations.keys()),
      366 +        }
      367 +
      368 +        # ── Homepage ──
      369 +        render_page(env, "index.html", lang_dir / "index.html", ctx)
      370 +
      371 +        # ── Calendar section ──
      372 +        cal_dir = lang_dir / "calendar"
      373 +        cal_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)
      374 +        render_page(env, "calendar/index.html", cal_dir / "index.html", ctx)
      375 +

      377 +        dp_dir = cal_dir / "datepicker"
      378 +        dp_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)
      379 +        render_page(env, "calendar/datepicker.html", dp_dir / "index.html", ctx)
      380 +

      382 +        ge_dir = cal_dir / "gaian-era"
      383 +        ge_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)
      384 +        render_page(env, "calendar/gaian-era.html", ge_dir / "index.html", ctx)
      385 +
      386 +        # Year page
      387 +        year_dir = cal_dir / "12026"
      388 +        year_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)
      389 +        render_page(env, "calendar/year.html", year_dir / "index.html",
      390 +                    {**ctx, "display_year": 12026})
      391 +

      394 +        week_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)
      395 +        render_page(env, "calendar/week-index.html", week_dir / "index.html",
      396 +                    {**ctx})
      397 +        for wd in WEEKDAYS:
      398 +            wd_dir = week_dir / wd["id"]
      399 +            wd_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)

      401 +                        {**ctx, "weekday": wd})
      402 +
      403 +        # ── Month pages ──
      404 +        for m in MONTHS:
      405 +            month_dir = cal_dir / m["id"]
      406 +            month_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)
      407 +            days_in_month = 7 if m["num"] == 14 else 28
      408 +            wiki_month = month_wiki_content.get(m["id"], {})
      409 +            month_ctx = {
      410 +                **ctx,

      436 +                }

      455 +                "chapter_day": ch_day,
      456 +                "prev_chapter": ch_num - 1 if ch_num > 1 else None,
      457 +                "next_chapter": ch_num + 1 if ch_num < 364 else None,
      458 +            }
      459 +            render_page(env, "gaiad/chapter.html", ch_dir / "index.html", ch_ctx)
      460 +

      463 +            sec_dir = lang_dir / section
      464 +            sec_dir.mkdir(exist_ok=True)
      465 +            render_page(env, f"sections/{section}.html", sec_dir / "index.html", ctx)
      466 +

      469 +                {"languages": list(translations.keys()), "translations": translations})

      476 +    total = sum(1 for _ in SITE_DIR.rglob("*.html"))
      477 +    print(f"Total HTML pages generated: {total}")
      478 +
      479 +
      480 +def render_page(env, template_name, output_path, context):
      481 +    """Render a Jinja2 template to a file."""
      482 +    output_path.parent.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)
      483 +    try:
      484 +        template = env.get_template(template_name)
      485 +        html = template.render(**context)
      486 +        with open(output_path, "w", encoding="utf-8") as f:
      487 +            f.write(html)
      488 +    except Exception as e:
      489 +        print(f"  ERROR rendering {template_name}: {e}")
      490 +
      491 +
      492 +if __name__ == "__main__":
      493 +    build_site()
